<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Titanium</h1>			<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">

				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the chemical element. <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_titanium" title="Template:Infobox titanium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_titanium" title="Template talk:Infobox titanium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_titanium&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div>&#x20;|&#x20;<a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Titanium</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Ti</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 22. It is a lustrous <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metal</a> with a silver color, low density, and high strength. Titanium is resistant to <a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">corrosion</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sea_water" class="mw-redirect" title="Sea water">sea water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aqua_regia" title="Aqua regia">aqua regia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>.
</p><p>Titanium was discovered in <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, by <a href="/wiki/William_Gregor" title="William Gregor">William Gregor</a> in 1791, and was named by <a href="/wiki/Martin_Heinrich_Klaproth" title="Martin Heinrich Klaproth">Martin Heinrich Klaproth</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)">Titans</a> of <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>. The element occurs within a number of <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">mineral</a> deposits, principally <a href="/wiki/Rutile" title="Rutile">rutile</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ilmenite" title="Ilmenite">ilmenite</a>, which are widely distributed in the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_crust" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth&#39;s crust">Earth's crust</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">lithosphere</a>, and it is found in almost all living things, water bodies, rocks, and soils.<sup id="cite_ref-EBC_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBC-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The metal is extracted from its principal mineral ores by the <a href="/wiki/Kroll_process" title="Kroll process">Kroll</a><sup id="cite_ref-LANL_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Hunter_process" title="Hunter process">Hunter processes</a>. The most common compound, <a href="/wiki/Titanium_dioxide" title="Titanium dioxide">titanium dioxide</a>, is a popular <a href="/wiki/Photocatalysis" title="Photocatalysis">photocatalyst</a> and is used in the manufacture of white pigments.<sup id="cite_ref-HistoryAndUse_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HistoryAndUse-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Other compounds include <a href="/wiki/Titanium_tetrachloride" title="Titanium tetrachloride">titanium tetrachloride</a> (TiCl<sub>4</sub>), a component of <a href="/wiki/Smoke_screen" title="Smoke screen">smoke screens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Catalyst" class="mw-redirect" title="Catalyst">catalysts</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Titanium(III)_chloride" title="Titanium(III) chloride">titanium trichloride</a> (TiCl<sub>3</sub>), which is used as a catalyst in the production of <a href="/wiki/Polypropylene" title="Polypropylene">polypropylene</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EBC_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBC-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Titanium can be <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloyed</a> with <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vanadium" title="Vanadium">vanadium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a>, among other elements, to produce strong, lightweight alloys for aerospace (<a href="/wiki/Jet_engine" title="Jet engine">jet engines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Missile" title="Missile">missiles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft" title="Spacecraft">spacecraft</a>), military, industrial processes (chemicals and petrochemicals, <a href="/wiki/Desalination_plant" class="mw-redirect" title="Desalination plant">desalination plants</a>, pulp, and paper), automotive, agri-food, medical <a href="/wiki/Prostheses" class="mw-redirect" title="Prostheses">prostheses</a>, orthopedic <a href="/wiki/Implant_(medicine)" title="Implant (medicine)">implants</a>, dental and endodontic instruments and files, <a href="/wiki/Dental_implant" title="Dental implant">dental implants</a>, sporting goods, jewelry, <a href="/wiki/Mobile_phone" title="Mobile phone">mobile phones</a>, and other applications.<sup id="cite_ref-EBC_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBC-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The two most useful properties of the metal are corrosion resistance and strength-to-density ratio, the highest of any metallic element.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In its unalloyed condition, titanium is as strong as some <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steels</a>, but less dense.<sup id="cite_ref-Barksdale1968p738_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barksdale1968p738-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> There are two <a href="/wiki/Allotropy" title="Allotropy">allotropic</a> forms<sup id="cite_ref-TICE6th_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TICE6th-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> and five naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a> of this element, <sup>46</sup>Ti through <sup>50</sup>Ti, with <sup>48</sup>Ti being the most <a href="/wiki/Natural_abundance" title="Natural abundance">abundant</a> (73.8%).<sup id="cite_ref-EnvChem_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EnvChem-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Although they have the same number of <a href="/wiki/Valence_electron" title="Valence electron">valence electrons</a> and are in the same <a href="/wiki/Group_(periodic_table)" title="Group (periodic table)">group</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>, titanium and <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">zirconium</a> differ in many chemical and physical properties.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span></h3>
<p>As a <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a>, titanium is recognized for its high <a href="/wiki/Strength-to-weight_ratio" class="mw-redirect" title="Strength-to-weight ratio">strength-to-weight ratio</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-TICE6th_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TICE6th-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> It is a strong metal with low <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> that is quite <a href="/wiki/Ductility" title="Ductility">ductile</a> (especially in an <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>-free environment),<sup id="cite_ref-EBC_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBC-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> lustrous, and metallic-white in <a href="/wiki/Color" title="Color">color</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Stwertka1998_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stwertka1998-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The relatively high melting point (more than 1,650&#160;°C or 3,000&#160;°F) makes it useful as a <a href="/wiki/Refractory_metals" title="Refractory metals">refractory metal</a>. It is <a href="/wiki/Paramagnetism" title="Paramagnetism">paramagnetic</a> and has fairly low <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EBC_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBC-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Commercially pure (99.2% pure) <a href="/wiki/Titanium_alloy#Grades_of_titanium" title="Titanium alloy">grades</a> of titanium have <a href="/wiki/Ultimate_tensile_strength" title="Ultimate tensile strength">ultimate tensile strength</a> of about 434 <a href="/wiki/Megapascal" class="mw-redirect" title="Megapascal">MPa</a> (63,000 <a href="/wiki/Pounds_per_square_inch" title="Pounds per square inch">psi</a>), equal to that of common, low-grade steel alloys, but are less dense. Titanium is 60% denser than aluminium, but more than twice as strong<sup id="cite_ref-Barksdale1968p738_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barksdale1968p738-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> as the most commonly used <a href="/wiki/6061_aluminium_alloy" title="6061 aluminium alloy">6061-T6 aluminium alloy</a>. Certain titanium alloys (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Titanium_Beta_C" title="Titanium Beta C">Beta C</a>) achieve tensile strengths of over 1,400 MPa (200,000 psi).<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> However, titanium loses strength when heated above 430&#160;°C (806&#160;°F).<sup id="cite_ref-Barksdale1968p734_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barksdale1968p734-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Titanium is not as hard as some grades of heat-treated steel; it is non-magnetic and a poor conductor of heat and electricity. Machining requires precautions, because the material can <a href="/wiki/Galling" title="Galling">gall</a> unless sharp tools and proper cooling methods are used. Like steel structures, those made from titanium have a <a href="/wiki/Fatigue_limit" title="Fatigue limit">fatigue limit</a> that guarantees longevity in some applications.<sup id="cite_ref-Stwertka1998_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stwertka1998-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The metal is a dimorphic <a href="/wiki/Allotropy" title="Allotropy">allotrope</a> of an hexagonal α form that changes into a body-centered cubic (lattice) β form at 882&#160;°C (1,620&#160;°F).<sup id="cite_ref-Barksdale1968p734_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barksdale1968p734-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Specific_heat_capacity" class="mw-redirect" title="Specific heat capacity">specific heat</a> of the α form increases dramatically as it is heated to this transition temperature but then falls and remains fairly constant for the β form regardless of temperature.<sup id="cite_ref-Barksdale1968p734_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barksdale1968p734-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_properties">Chemical properties</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Titanium_in_water_Pourbaix_diagram.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Titanium_in_water_Pourbaix_diagram.png/220px-Titanium_in_water_Pourbaix_diagram.png" width="220" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Titanium_in_water_Pourbaix_diagram.png/330px-Titanium_in_water_Pourbaix_diagram.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Titanium_in_water_Pourbaix_diagram.png/440px-Titanium_in_water_Pourbaix_diagram.png 2x" data-file-width="497" data-file-height="511" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Titanium_in_water_Pourbaix_diagram.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Pourbaix_diagram" title="Pourbaix diagram">Pourbaix diagram</a> for titanium in pure water, perchloric acid, or sodium hydroxide<sup id="cite_ref-medusa_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-medusa-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Like <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, titanium metal and its alloys <a href="/wiki/Oxidize" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxidize">oxidize</a> immediately upon exposure to air. Titanium readily reacts with oxygen at 1,200&#160;°C (2,190&#160;°F) in air, and at 610&#160;°C (1,130&#160;°F) in pure oxygen, forming <a href="/wiki/Titanium_dioxide" title="Titanium dioxide">titanium dioxide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-TICE6th_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TICE6th-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> It is, however, slow to react with water and air at ambient temperatures because it forms a <a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">passive</a> oxide coating that protects the bulk metal from further oxidation.<sup id="cite_ref-EBC_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EBC-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> When it first forms, this protective layer is only 1–2 <a href="/wiki/Nanometre" title="Nanometre">nm</a> thick but continues to grow slowly; reaching a thickness of 25&#160;nm in four years.<sup id="cite_ref-Emsley2001p453_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2001p453-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Atmospheric passivation gives titanium excellent resistance to corrosion, almost equivalent to <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a>. Titanium is capable of withstanding attack by dilute <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acids</a>, chloride solutions, and most organic acids.<sup id="cite_ref-LANL_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> However, titanium is corroded by concentrated acids.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> As indicated by its negative redox potential, titanium is thermodynamically a very reactive metal that burns in normal atmosphere at lower temperatures than the melting point. Melting is possible only in an inert atmosphere or in a vacuum. At 550&#160;°C (1,022&#160;°F), it combines with chlorine.<sup id="cite_ref-LANL_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> It also reacts with the other halogens and absorbs hydrogen.<sup id="cite_ref-HistoryAndUse_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HistoryAndUse-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Titanium is one of the few elements that burns in pure nitrogen gas, reacting at 800&#160;°C (1,470&#160;°F) to form <a href="/wiki/Titanium_nitride" title="Titanium nitride">titanium nitride</a>, which causes embrittlement.<sup id="cite_ref-titaniumindustry_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-titaniumindustry-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Because of its high reactivity with oxygen, nitrogen, and some other gases, titanium <a href="/wiki/Electrical_filament" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrical filament">filaments</a> are applied in <a href="/wiki/Titanium_sublimation_pump" title="Titanium sublimation pump">titanium sublimation pumps</a> as scavengers for these gases. Such pumps inexpensively and reliably produce extremely low pressures in <a href="/wiki/Ultra-high_vacuum" title="Ultra-high vacuum">ultra-high vacuum</a> systems.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span></h3>
